B000003CVX|5.0|I introduced myself to Richard Strauss's work when I purchased the recordings of David Zinman and the Zurich Tonhalle Orchestra. I noticed right away that R. Strauss was a master of Late Romantic Classical Music. The composer had created some timeless melodies and complex orchestral structures. Don Juan, Tod und Verkl&auml;rung (Death and Transfiguration), and Festliches Pr&auml;ludium (Festival Prelude) are prime examples of his apparent intelligence. Eine Alpensinfonie (An Alpine Symphony) was composed in 1915, a time when he was at the peak of his career. It is one of his most programmatic works, and requires over a hundred players. It is musically complex, and yet it's quite colorful and tremendously explosive. Although the work itself a bit of a challenge because of the number of players required, it has found itself a wide discography. Numerous conductors such as Karl Bohm and Neeme Jarvi have accepted the challenge and, as a result, have been praised for their own renditions with the orchestras that they have chosen. My personal favorite is still the Zinman recording, the first one that I've ever purchased. However, it looks like Zinman has found a worthy adversary: Andr&eacute; Previn.<br /><br />With the ever-popular Vienna Philharmonic, Previn pays as much attention as Zinman. The only difference is that Previn adds more energy into the piece. He displays more emotion as he superbly captures the imagery of the Alps: from the rivers to the glaciers to even the cow & its cowbell. The Vienna Philharmonic's performance is just dazzling. The brass are heard loud and clear, the woodwinds never lose their delicate touch, and the strings play with the right amount of power and blissful lyricism. There are some faults to Previn's recording, however. The \\"Obscured Sun\\" and \\"Elegy\\" are a bit rushed, and the soft percussion is barely heard in some moments, if not heard at all. Some of the small orchestral details that are found in Zinman's recording are lost in Previn's, mostly because of the larger \\"echoes\\" in the acoustics. In the end, when intensity is gained, musical clarity is lost.<br /><br />If I had to choose between Zinman and Previn, I would go for the former. Zinman's recording is more moderately measured, and yet the clarity and the effective choice of tempos are appropriate. But even if Previn's recording of Eine Alpensinfonie is a bit flawed, it still has a lot to offer. If you want a purely energetic Alpensinfonie, then Previn is the one to buy.<br /><br />Grade: 8.7 out of 10e larger \\"echoes\\" in the acoustics. In the end, when intensity is gained, musical clarity is lost.  If I had to choose between Zinman and Previn, I would go for the former. Zinman's recording is more moderately measured, and yet the clarity and the effective choice of tempos are appropriate. But even if Previn's recording of Eine Alpensinfonie is a bit flawed, it still has a lot to offer. If you want a purely energetic Alpensinfonie, then Previn is the one to buy.  Grade: 8.7 out of 10

0307474259|3.0|I first read Good Calories and Bad Calories and liked how well-annotated it was, and was not put off by its length. This book suffers from being too terse at times. A lot of assertions are made with minimal footnotes, so I had a harder time checking the sources. Still, I am in the process of following up on some of the claims and studies examined, and I am beginning to see that some of the conclusions drawn are not valid.  Example 1: The Cochrane review from 2001 that says there is insufficient data to conclude that lowering fat or saturated fat is healthy is irrelevant in lieu of a more recent Cochrane review indicating that there is evidence that lowering saturated fat is healthy.  Example 2: If you look up the A to Z Stanford study, you'll find that many researchers don't think its conclusions are valid because the subjects assigned to different diet groups did not follow the diet's guidelines. For example, the Atkins group ate too many carbs and were likely not in ketosis, and the Ornish group ate way too many total and saturated fats.  Example 3: Taubes argues that the obesity epidemic coincides with public health advice to eat less fat, but it ignores the increased incidence of corn syrup consumption, which recent research shows is metabolized differently than sucrose and is therefore more fattening even in smaller amounts. This shift to corn syrup (HFCS in particular) coincides with greater portions because corn syrup is less expensive and food can therefore be sold for less money. Instead of doing this, food manufacturers sell more food for slightly more money so people think they're getting a good deal.  The point is that refined carbohydrates are more of a problem than carbohydrates in general. Taubes's arguments aren't all invalid, but in light of the other ways in which the American diet has changed since the 70s, the conclusions he draws aren't cogent, nor is all the research cited unquestionable, and it's intellectually dishonest of him to be so thorough about picking apart the research that indicts saturated fat while pretending that the research on which his arguments are drawn don't also suffer from methodological flaws.  In the end, I have decided to limit carbohydrates across the board, but I'm examining the evidence to determine whether I'll stay on a ketogenic diet, and whether I can eat unrestricted amounts of saturated fat. If you read this book, follow up on the sources on which he makes his biggest claims.

B0064NTZQ2|5.0|Although I loved this movie, I also understand why so many people don't like it. The pacing is deliberately slow, building up tension for a brutal climax. Many people will see this as boring, being so accustomed to non-stop action & explosions that are supposedly the only testament to a movie being \\"exciting.\\" But this movie is all about leading up to this action; it's truly a story with many layers. In the place of filler dialogue, Gosling uses facial expressions to portray his emotions and set the movie's tone. In place of mindless and ridiculously over-the-top \\"action\\" that most movies substitute in place of an actual storyline, this movie builds on its plot and fully develops the characters before the shocking and graphic second half.<br /><br />The first half tries to prepare us (and even brace us) for what is to come; this tactic makes the second half intensely realistic and therefore all the more unsettling. I could write all day about the effective use of symbolism and foreshadowing (Driver asking Benicio if there are any good \\"sharks\\" and the subsequent handling of one of those \\"sharks\\" right after the T-bone car accident; Driver asking Bernie if he's ever heard of the story of the scorpion and the frog while wearing a scorpion jacket, giving us insight as to how he views himself) or the many interpretations of the movie's title (not just about his profession, of course).<br /><br />This movie doesn't give you everything on-screen; you have to dig deeper to figure some things out for yourself. It challenges us to be a smarter audience and to let the story naturally unfold on its own. B005FNJL7K|4.0|As someone who purchased all three theatrical soundtracks, purchased all three complete recordings, and owns Symphony No. 1 by de Meij, you could easily classify me as a fan of Lord of the Rings music.  When I discovered this I was intrigued and I had questions.  How could one possibly reduce that many hours of glorious, soul stopping music into a six movement piece that still did it justice?  Well, Howard Shore has done so (almost).  I must say, being so devoted to the film music, I was a little nervous to listen to this.  However, I was pleasantly surprised.  I actually listened to this via youtube video (if you're curious like me I would suggest looking it up), and my first thought after a few bars was \\"Wow!  This is cool!\\"  I did however notice differences that were disappointing.  One thing is certain, there isn't quite the same \\"OOMPH!\\" behind the music as there is in the film music.  In this piece the vocals, as well as instrumentation, were a bit hollow sounding.  Not that they didn't sound great, but it just wasn't the same power as the score.  I would describe it, as my title says, as a new flavor of the LOTR music.  Familiar themes, good performances, but just not quite the same power.  When I put on my headphones to listen to the film music or to de Meij's Symphony No. 1, it's as if I'm transported to Middle Earth and the music soaks into my very soul.  This, however, did not illicit the same reaction.  One of my favorite themes in the film music (and de Meij) is the Journey Through the Dark/Mines of Moria/Bridge of Khazad-d√ªm; the music literally is so beautiful to me it brings me to tears.  My second favorite themes are the ones we encounter in Lothlorien.  Sorely disappointed with that one in this recording.  The chord hit at 44 seconds on the track \\"Caras Galadhon\\" on complete recordings gives me chills every time; not so here. The same themes in this are just lack-luster compared to the other incarnations.  Overall the pacing of this seemed way toofast in places throughout the piece.  Overall, I give this a 4/5 because I like it, but I do not love it.  I do not plan on purchasing, however, I will enjoy that  youtube video from time to time I'm sure.  It is a great piece, but considering there are the soundtracks and complete recordings out there, this pales in comparison.  Perhaps if I had not yet heard the other music I would give this a more positive review, but I can't put the other music out of my mind.  It is still Howard Shore and Lord of the Rings, just not as good as the real thing.  If anything, I thank this recording for deepening my love of the complete recordings/theatrical soundtracks.  (On a rather random side note, try playing tracks simultaneously.  It creates this awesome round-robin effect if you get it just right! Lothlorien and Foundations of Stone were both pretty awesome!)

HOWEVER, I do think the author is overly enamored with the French way of doing things, especially since she oohs and aahs over the basic parenting techniques they use that are also used by most conservative parents in the U.S.: Hello? Saying hello and good-bye to guests is somehow revolutionary? I thought it was just basic manners. Not hitting your parents? Understanding that \\"no\\" means no and that parents who don't say it with authority are basically being laughed at not only by their disobedient kids but also by the adults around them?  We do not let rude children come to play in our house... period! We do everything we can to encourage our kids' relationships with like-minded kids and their families.  Having said that, I was particularly interested in the way children are fed in France, and that seemed to coincide with the style of eating I experienced while there. But being shocked that kids sit at the table and eat with their family? I don't think so!  Also, I was more than a little unnerved by the author's seeming love affair with the socialistic French society that aims to groom little French statesmen from the earliest of years (through government daycare). Also, you'll notice that while she describes how great it is to have socialized medicine, she also notes the difference in care between public and private hospitals, having delivered children in both. I gave birth under socialized medicine as well (in Canada, where private hospital care is illegal) and can tell you that there are few things more horrific.  My last comment is one of wonder at how the supposedly \\"middle class\\" parents in France that the author describes somehow have enough money for full-time round the clock nannies and multiple apartments in Paris that they hire architects to renovate for them. This is hardly the same middle class existence we find here in the States. And while not letting children be the center of the universe and control your life is a good thing, I'm sorry but farming your kids out to strangers so that you can have \\"adult time\\" does not constitute my idea of a good parent.  All in all it's an interesting read, but just know that you need to use your critical thinking skills here and not take everything at face value.

0312641893|4.0|A futuristic cyborg retelling of Cinderella sounds outrageous, but Marissa Meyer really takes the timeless tale and makes it her own with CINDER, first of a four-book series. Imaginative and action-packed, with likable characters, CINDER will appeal to readers looking for a dose of creative adventure.<br /><br />In the future city of New Beijing, Cinder works as a talented young mechanic, but her status as a cyborg and ward of her stepmother means that she is trapped. When a mysterious and deadly plague touches her family, Cinder is thrown into the paths of members of the royal palace, including the handsome and caring Prince Kai. There may be something about Cinder that will be the key to finding a cure for the plague, but will Cinder ever be regarded by anyone as more than simply a cyborg?<br /><br />Where do I start in gushing about how enjoyable CINDER was? Cinder the protagonist was a lovely protagonist. She is smart, resourceful, and brave. Cinder belongs in that small group of YA heroines who do not possess any extraordinary survival tactics and yet is a fighter: she deals with her troubles in a way that many of us can imagine us doing if we were ever in her position.<br /><br />My next point is contentious, as many great reviewers have felt this part underdeveloped, but I felt that CINDER did a great job of creating a unique future world. No, it's not quite a dystopian, and it's not \\"hard\\" sci-fi: if it were either of those, the world's connections to our present-day world would need to be more convincing. However, I was able to enjoy CINDER's setting of New Beijing as almost a fantasy world, set on a planet with a similar layout to ours, with new technology but similar problems of politics and society. Story and setting complemented each other well: the plot never dragged in order to appease world-building, and the world-building was enough such that the story was supported well.<br /><br />Overall, CINDER may not win any literary awards in the near future, but it is an utterly enjoyable retelling of a classic in a futuristic fantasy world, with recognizable elements from the old tale but enough new elements to keep things fresh. I was surprised at the abruptness of the ending, until I learned that the Lunar Chronicles is a four-part exploration all about Cinder and her adventures, not separate stories each. Oh. Okay. There will be resolutions in the future, then! 0316066524|5.0|I'm ordinarily a pretty fast reader, so I imagined I would be able to knock this out in a week or two. Boy, was I wrong. And I have to say I've seldom been happier to be wrong. Infinite Jest is one of those books that takes time to get it's hooks fully into you, but when it does it's hard not to be completely in thrall to it. I'm not even sure it's a novel as much as it is a holistic intellectual commitment the way most German Philosophers and some long-running HBO television series are. I found myself actively wondering about the characters, the structure, the chronology, the footnotes, and in general just puzzling over this bizarre, fractured little world like it was my own. Wallace has syntactical prowess out to *here* and I feel like you don't read these sentences as much as you just navigate them and sort of pull them through with you, filling in the little ellipses, argots and inside jokes as you go along. And yet in spite (or perhaps because) of all of the rich stylistic exploration, Wallace is able to create compelling, humane characters, often in the span of just a few pages. And what's more, is often able to make them so real so valid, and often so sad, that they could almost wander off and become little novels of their own. It IS worth the effort.

1439156816|4.0|I've never read King before, and this is obviously not representative of his work. What it is however, is a pretty decent mini-memoir/writing guide which covers the basics of both categories without wallowing unnecessarily in either. I always think of King as this interminably successful pop author. I can literally never recall a point in my life when I didn't walk into a bookstore and see a new (or reprinted) Steven King bestseller somewhere. So it was really interesting reading about his early years basically just being this working class 20 something guy living in different parts of rural Maine. And anyone who could support a wife and two kids at that age working an endless succession of dead end jobs and still have time left over to write anything at all is not someone you can entirely dismiss out of hand. If he comes across as a bit too cocky, it's also quickly obvious that its a sort of cockiness he's earned and at least he doesn't rub your face in it.  The sections on writing itself seemed pretty basic. Not Strunk and White soulless beige cubicle basic, but basic to anyone whose ever sat through multiple composition/creative writing classes (guilty as charged). But then most really good writing advice is. And the voice he uses is engaging enough to make you not care when he's stating the dumbly obvious. By far the best section is towards the end when he discusses the auto accident that nearly killed him in '99. It walks this really fine line between being gruesome, poignant and funny. And that's hard to pull off in fiction, much less when writing about real life. It actually made me want to sit down and bother to read some of his books. Oh yeah, and it made me want to write too.

0307888908|5.0|Steve Jobs once called Apple the \\"biggest start-up on the planet\\". Because of its success at systemizing disruptive innovation, it is the most scrutinized company in the world, especially by its competitors. As secretive as Apple is, there are plenty of books and articles that examine Apple's culture in great detail. So why are most companies -- especially large, established corporations -- incapable of applying these principles themselves?  I bring up this question because the first time I read this book, I was disappointed. So many of the strategies seemed so intuitive, so obvious, that I didn't feel like I had gleaned as much insight as I had hoped. But as I looked over my highlights, I realized that, like Apple's competitors, I'd missed the point.  What you need to adapt to the changing world of work aren't cheap tactics and off-the-wall ideas and quick fixes to hack your career. Often, the best ideas *do* seem obvious and familiar. But what is less obvious and less familiar and, thus, is arguably more important and more useful, is a framework or blueprint or system that makes it easier to build the habits required to consistently and sustainably implement those ideas with every decision you make, day in and day out.  The framework this book offers is this: you must think and act like you're running a start-up.  But what does that mean? Here's an example. One deceptively simple philosophy in the book is the idea of helping first -- that you should find ways to create value for others before seeking value for yourself. It's so simple and obvious that it's easy to gloss over it, and yet, it is fundamental to every successful entrepreneur or start-up in the world, because you'll never get a single customer until you solve a problem for someone.  So how does one apply this philosophy? Here's what Reid does: whenever he gets the chance, he asks a simple question again and again and again: \\"How can I help?\\" Think about it: It sounds simple, and yet most people don't approach their careers or their relationships with this empathetic mindset. How often do you find ways to solve problems for the people around you? And if it isn't often, how do you change that? What habits does Reid have that you need to do this, too?  \\"The Start-up of You\\" will give you Reid Hoffman's -- and Silicon Valley's -- secret sauce, but it's not enough to know it, just like it isn't enough for Apple's competitors to know its philosophies. You have to understand that an entrepreneurial mindset requires different habits of thought and action. This is what the authors mean when they say you have to think and act like you're running a start-up. If you were running a start-up, how would you create a culture that instills the habits of thought and action you want your people to have?  Well, it turns out you *are* running a start-up: your career. How do you train yourself to have the habits of thought and action you need to thrive in the 21st century?  Or, put another way, what would an entrepreneur do?

0525422943|4.0|With multi-platinum records, a celebrity girlfriend, and fame galore, rocker Adam Wilde appears to have it all. But from the surface no one would know how close he is to unraveling. He is addicted to a myriad of substances and his relationship with the band is strained to the point that they don't even stay in the same hotels.  These pale in comparison to the root of the problem, the absence of Mia in his life. For all he can see, it seems as if Mia hasn't blinked in the three years they've been apart. Studying at Julliard while her concert-career takes off, she's probably too busy and too happy in her success to miss him. But when Adam and Mia are reunited in New York, they are forced to confront what staying has cost each of them, and where they've been. Maybe twenty-four hours is all they needed for closure from the past and hope for the future.  In all honesty I didn't rush out to read Where She Went after I finished If I Stay (my review). As fitting as the ending was to Mia's ordeal, I wasn't sure where her story could go that would satisfy ending on the note that it did, at the beginning of a path of grief and pain. But just knowing the sequel was out there with a possible happy ending for these characters was irresistible. Even if it wasn't the ending I envisioned I had to know anyway, especially in light of the glowing reviews and better-than-the-first declarations in the blogosphere. I'm very glad that I eventually picked it up - if I had known what I was missing, I would have regretted not doing so.  There is something to say about quick reads that have substance; but whether it was quick or slow, heavy or light, I could not put down Gayle Forman's Where She Went. The spare prose is even more sure of itself here, with the narration switching to Adam after such a dramatic turn of events. And with the exceptional characterization on display I could not be more captivated. Broken and messed up as he is, I may like Adam more than Mia in If I Stay if only for how his whole soul is laid out. Ms. Forman spares us nothing. In his unhappiness, Adam's behavior is ugly at times, from lashing out at reporters to chewing out his band mates and simply being a privileged jerk. Anyone can see that he just needs love and friendship, so you can't hate him for his actions. I loved him all the more for what he's gone through and how he's grown up. For all the information given for where Adam has been, we offered about the opposite for Mia. Yes, she's been beyond busy studying at Julliard, practicing, and traveling around the world performing, but where has she been emotionally? I would have appreciated some of the novel written from her perspective. As it is, it seemed out of character for her to drop Adam the way she does without further explanation. Nevertheless, it's hard to judge the girl after all she has lost. The ease of the solution seemed to fit the heartbreaking circumstances. After reading the ending I happily flipped back to reread those quiet, intimate moments at Mia's apartment that made my heart palpitate. Both If I Stay and Where She Went are beautifully written novels that will do just about anything that can be done to your heart. A worthy sequel, Where She Went is recommended for fans of contemporary YA.

0439813786|4.0|For a boy in 1931 Paris, Hugo Cabret leads an unusual life. Living alone in the train station, he gets by by keeping the clocks wound and stealing. Whether it's food or small mechanical parts, stealing has become second nature to him. Possessing those cogs and wheels means the world to Hugo, so when getting caught by the old man at the toy booth puts a damper on his collecting, Hugo sees it as the end of it. But then there's the girl - maybe she can help free him from his predicament. He couldn't foresee that she and the man would be much more than that as he searches for meaning and purpose in his solitary life.  I'm not sure why I held off on experiencing Brian Selznick's The Invention of Hugo Cabret until now. In retrospect it seems right up my alley with its unique and novel format.  I knew that it used both words and illustrations to tell its story, and with it winning the Caldecott Medal I expected to see elements of a picture book. The graphic novel similarities were also expected, as the book relies strictly on images to tell portions of the story in certain sections. What surprised me was how from the start it also felt like a film - a silent, black and white one at that - or even one with stop animation. Reading further the references to early film history made it clear how well the media suited the story. It is how Hugo bends and blurs the lines between these mediums which is my favorite aspect of the book. What I also loved (and made the format work) was how smooth and natural the transitions from words to pictures felt. Most often it was the illustrated action scenes which I found so refreshing and appropriate. After all sometimes a picture does speak a thousand words. Beyond my enjoyment of the format, I liked Hugo, who is flawed in his good intentions. Life may not turn out like he planned but what happened is better than he could ever have dreamed.  I enjoyed the simple, heartfelt story of how he becomes the Hugo he is today. A quick yet meaningful winter read, The Invention of Hugo Cabret was exactly what I needed this busy time of year. I will definitely be picking up Selznick's latest Wonderstruck, which I already have in my possession.

B0012GMXYM|5.0|With the massive creative and even critical success of [[ASIN:B0000A2I1B Caravanserai]] and[[ASIN:B0012GMV58 Welcome]] in the last couple years before this,Carlos Santana and his band weren't exactly gaining any huge commercial successes from any of these. Whenever they appeared on a TV show or in concert,it seemed they were always expected to play the bigger hit songs from their debut trio of albums. Those were as creative hits and albums as they'd ever done. But when they moved beyond that,more towards jazz and funk they really began to lose some of that attention. They were perceived as a psychedelic latin rock band. The reality is...there was a lot more happening. It had to do with the San Francisco culture they spawned from. With Tom Coster still onboard for this album and Miles Davis/Chick Corea alumni Airto and Flora Purim taking a bigger role this time out this album musically really clicked with everything they'd been coming at since they started their career. I think it probably went somewhat unnoticed to even some hardcore fans. And I happen to know some writers and critics kind of dismissed it as...forgettable. Well whatever it is,forgettable it isn't.<br /><br />  Even with albums in between this provides something of the musical link between the earlier [[ASIN:B0000A2I1B Caravanserai]] and the next album [[ASIN:B00171RQBW Amigos]]. Starting out with \\"Spring Manifestations\\" on a Brazillian fusion percussion roll call or sorts and ending in the same manner on the closing title song,with Airto's wordless vocal cries as an exit point we have instrumental numbers such as the late 60's Miles Davis type,quietly melodic \\"Conto De Los Flores\\" along with \\"Aspirations\\",the leslie amped \\"Flor De Canela\\" and \\"Promises Of A Fisherman\\". These are all melody based,filled with this almost...audio impression of bright sunshine directly overhead. Quiet but stinging hot all at the same time. Tom Coster's organ work is paramount on these songs. \\"Life Is Anew\\",\\"Give And Take\\",\\"One With The Sun\\",\\"Practice What You Preach\\",\\"Mirage\\" and \\"Here And Now\\" make up the main core of the albums. All are vocal numbers featuring the relatively newbie Christian funk vocalist Leon Patillo,who sings very meaningful and spiritually open lyrics along with some of the most flowing,slick and danceable melodic funk of the era. Each starting off in the somewhat more jazzy vein these numbers are truly Ricky Vincent's \\"united funk era\\" personified.<br /><br />  This newfound funk direction would be something admirers would hear the band continue to develop for the rest of the 70's decade. They would soon get their poppier side going stong again after this album. And this would wind up being the last of the Santana band's more abstract jazzier style excursions. But this one kind of one up's it's predecessor to a degree by rather anticipating a more pop oriented sound with it's thick but softly produced funk grooves. Nothing on this albums,even Carlos's passionate as always lead solos are particularly loud. In a lot of ways,aside from the sometimes penetrating sound of the guitar solos this isn't even a rock n' roll album in the traditional sense of the word. Not even psychedelic. The songs are deep inside the groove and there really isn't the same sense of ego tripping noise making usually at the core of rock music. Maybe it was some type of rock in a jazzier funk place,who knows. If so Santana was continuing to redefine what rock was. I heard it said recently rock music was at something of a low during 1974 in particular. Maybe because with albums like this it was starting to merely turn different colors that got some people a little confused. Either way this is an excellent album,great to get your head and ears straightened out with and revisiting if you never heard it.th The Sun\\",\\"Practice What You Preach\\",\\"Mirage\\" and \\"Here And Now\\" make up the main core of the albums. B006UG90RM|3.0|I would definitely liken this release to a mix of good and bad. Some songs work, some don't. But, the biggest problem with this release is the production.  Specifically, Eddie's tone.  I was full well expecting Eddie to bring back some semblance of his original signature brown guitar tone.  Instead, this album completely went the other way.  The guitar tone is muddy, muddled and crunchy (in a bad way).  It doesn't even sound like the Van Hagar era tone either. The only way we know this is Eddie is through his guitar phrasing. Unfortuntely, the guitar was fed directly from his guitar processor right into the digital mixing board and then recorded. I should know, I've done this and it produces a tone that sounds just like this.<br /><br />Instead, with the return of DLR, I was fully hoping Eddie to air mic his Marshall amps once again and give us some sort of analog sounding tone. Instead, we get this.  And no, it is not a brown tone.<br /><br />Additionally, the songs don't really have the hooks that we would expect to hear in Van Halen songs, like Runnin' with the Devil or Jamie's Cryin.  It doesn't even fit the mold for today's pop or rock standards either.  This CD has more in common with a Jam session than an AOR rock CD.<br /><br />As for mastering, this CD just doesn't have the dynamics needed to make it stand out aurally. The mastering engineer just didn't do the final mastering correctly.  The drums are majorly buried (other than the snare) with Eddie's guitar practically in your face.  The mastering tone of the songs is very midrangey and flat.  The high ends are missing in the snares and the guitar.  Overall, this CD needs a lot of help to make it sonically better.<br /><br />Personally, I'd tear all the instrument tracks apart and redo the whole mastering job from the ground up.  I'd give the drums much more brilliance and depth.  I'd run Eddie's guitar through a cabinet emulator to at least sound like it came out of a Marshall.  I'd then put it all together and push the guitars back just a little so we can at least hear the drums and bass better.  Frankly, I might even move the dry guitar to the left side with the wet on the right (at least on some of the songs) in ode to the original VH mixes. Instead, the compilers have sought the best Furtwangler versions of each work, with excerpts from some of the alternative recordings. Hearing the conductors own symphonies is of more than passing interest.<br />Blemishes that should have been noticed during the digital transfers include an abrupt change in sound level during the slow movement of Tchaikovsky's 5th symphony and the clipping of the first note of the 3rd movement of Mozart's Symphony no. 40 - a fault that is also to be found on the EMI and Naxos transfers, but not on the original 78 rpm or LP versions. B000P0J09M|5.0|Interestingly, I rewatched Goodfellas, #94 on the AFI top 100, right after rewatching A Clockwork Orange, #46 on the AFI top 100. Both are about an enthusiasm for violence. Both tie music into what is going on with the plot. Watching them back to back, even accepting how \\"groundbreaking\\" A Clockwork Orange is with its alternate universe and its invented language, I still think Goodfellas should rank far higher than it. The acting, the plot, and the insight in Goodfellas is simply phenomenal.<br /><br />First, we start with the actors. In A Clockwork Orange there is Malcolm McDowell, and while he's good, I'm not sure one person alone can stand up to Robert De Niro, Lorraine Bracco, Ray Liotta, Joe Pesci, and the many other fantastic actors that are everywhere you turn in Goodfellas. Every single person is simply amazing. Even the \\"bit parts\\" like Michael Imperioli's \\"Spider\\" character get you interested. You are watching the very best of the best.<br /><br />And what they are playing is a real life story. This isn't an invention or fantasy. This really happened. These characters, their intrigues, their lies and deceptions and lust for violence, all of this was real. The men joking around with the mistress while the wife went to the family functions, the assumption that violence could happen at any time, this was the life they lived in.<br /><br />The music here is simply phenomenal. It sets you into the timeframe, and you can literally watch as the years unroll. It's not just the music, it's the hair styles, the cars, the decor of the rooms, everything. You can feel yourself reliving the decades and the feelings that they brought.<br /><br />The characters evolve over time. They rebel and feel angst and come to accept and then embrace what is happening around them.<br /><br />It's interesting because there's the same lust for violence here as in A Clockwork Orange. The characters enjoy pummeling another person. When Lorraine's character is roughed up by a neighborhood slick-guy, and Ray goes in to teach him a lesson, Ray *wants* to beat him up and put him in his place. When Joe feels disrespected in the bar, he wants to beat the guy into a bloody pulp, and he does. The violence is a normal, natural part of life.<br /><br />The women, unfortunately, hold the same be-used-and-often role in this movie as well. Women are there to be wives, kicking out kids and attending functions, or as mistresses, proving sex. In both cases they are supposed to be good natured, appreciate their part, and not attempt to change anything. A woman who complains at all about the small portion of life she's allowed can be beaten or worse.<br /><br />Still, I do at least give Goodfellas credit for giving part of the story through Lorraine's eyes. We see how hard it was for her, as the wife, to know there was a mistress around, that she \\"lost\\" her husband for days a week into that woman's arms, and that there was nothing she could do about it. She couldn't divorce, she couldn't complain. She just had to take it. The only other women she could ever talk with were other mob wives in the exact same situation. All the advice she got was \\"at least he's not in jail\\".<br /><br />However, in terms of the Bechdel test, it fails. The women rarely talk with each other, and when they do, it's about their men.<br /><br />A powerful look into a very real segment of society - and as much as it glamorizes how nice it was at times to be in a mob environment, it also shows the darker underside of it. While it might be fun while it lasted, it didn't necessarily last long.<br /><br />A great, powerful, well acted film. She couldn't divorce, she couldn't complain. She just had to take it. The only other women she could ever talk with were other mob wives in the exact same situation. B004K583KA|5.0|The Bach Cantatas unquestionably constitute the greatest single achievement by a single composer within a single genre in the history of Western music (though Haydn's cycle of Quartets runs it a close second in my estimation).  If you love classical music, sacred music, and Bach, you must not deprive yourself of the incredible privilege of getting to know this massive achievement. Perhaps even more mind-boggling than the proportion of quality to quantity in this vast output is the realization that there are probably at least a hundred more Cantatas that have not come down to us!  If there is a heaven--and it's hard to remain agnostic after a close encounter with these works--then I can only hope that it will be the privilege of blessed Bachians to hear these unknown Cantatas (and anything else the great master might have cobbled together on the slopes of Eternity).<br /><br />Returning to the present age for the moment, one notes with some dismay that the consolations of great music do not come without a cost--yes, the cost of intellectual effort, but also financial outlay.  And it is on this point that H&auml;nssler-Verlag's reissue of Rilling's Cantata Cycle maintains a distinct advantage, whatever it musical merits or vices.  With the Secular Cantatas included, along with exhaustive scholarly notes, texts and translations downloadable (and printable) from the CD-Rom included, this is a bargain that no collector can or should resist at the current asking price.  Even if you own other other Bach Cantata recordings in abundance, don't pass this up.<br /><br />The reason is simply that this is the ONLY complete Bach Cantata cycle performed with modern instruments, ample mixed choirs (not a countertenor among them!, and soloists accomplished in traditional (one may even call it \\"operatic\\") vocal technique.  Admittedly, a series of this magnitude, directed by a single conductor, and recorded over a span of at least two decades, will sometimes fall below expectations.  But the standard of accomplishment Rilling has achieved here is consistently high.  Though some of the earlier recordings are lacking in rhythmic vitality and favor too much legato phrasing, Rilling's priorities changed perceptibly during the first decade of this project, so that the bulk of these renditions are stylistically aware without being pedantic, energetic without being rushed, expressive without being romanticized. Indeed, if one compares Rilling's \\"learning curve\\" to that of Harnoncourt and Leonhardt in *their* pioneering Bach Cantata series--factoring out, for the moment, differences in performance practice priorities--one notes a conspicuous decline in the latter's engagement with the music, whereas one observes the opposite trend in Rilling's cycle.  In other words, Harnoncourt/Leonhardt evidently began to lose interest in the project after it was well underway, whereas Rilling's engagement never faltered, but only became more intense over time.<br /><br />This is obviously not the place to go into a detailed analysis of Rilling's performances.  I have been familiar with them, and impressed by them, since the days of vinyl discs.  Certain works, particularly the more festive ones, have rarely been surpassed for heady exuberance. One of my fellow reviewers cites BWV 129 as a salient example, and I readily concur. The opening chorus of BWV 100 provides another example of Rilling's ability to convey rapturous joy, and one could also cite the sparkling display of musical fireworks throughout the incomplete, and rarely heard, wedding cantata BWV 195. Rilling also frequently finds the charm in Bach's writing, particularly in those arias with obbligato winds; the delightful bass aria from BWV 123 provides a good example: the \\"walking bass\\" really really swings, with something like a detectable \\"back-beat,\\" while the flute solo enchants the ear with its florid passagework. Finally, I would note that at least one cantata--the well-known BWV 78--receives a rendition that is as close to \\"definitve\\" as any recorded Bach Cantata I know.  The famous duet zips along with enormous brio, and elsewhere the drama and pathos of this multi-diminsional score are perfectly conveyed.<br /><br />There are, of course, also interpretive misfires. The monumental opening chorus of BWV 102 bogs down into something of a sodden slog, and the four great choral pillars around which BWV 21 is constructed are similarly earthbound.  Occasionally, one finds Rilling insensitive to the affect of text and music, as in his brisk, mechanical treatment of the ineffable chorale setting with which BWV 105 concludes. That movement should begin in anxiety, and proceed through rejoicing to inner quietude; in Rilling's account, it remains boisterous all the way through. I could cite further examples in this vein, but fortunately for prospective purchasers, they are not numerous.<br /><br />Overall, then, the ratio of successes to failures, both in terms of entire works, and in terms of movements within cantatas, is remarkably high.  Of course, that positive outcome has as much to do with the quality of the vocal soloists (Helen Watts Arleen Auger and Phillipe Huttenlocher outstanding among them), choral singing (nice balance, good diction, sometimes too prominent vibrato) and ensemble playing (not quite in the class of Richter's Munich Bach Orchestra,but expert), as with Rilling's intelligent and unfussy direction.<br /><br />What of the recorded sound? Some of the earlier recordings in the series suffer from a rather shallow \\"soundstage\\" which, together with some close spotlighting of instrumental and vocal soloists, can create a cramped or harsh effect. Elsewhere, I noticed a persistent artificial-sounding resonance that might prove distracting on repetition. However, sonic imperfections scarcely detract from Rilling's achievement--the first single conductor to have run the Bach Cantata marathon--and H&auml;nssler's unbelievably low price tag.  As with the other reviewers I can only entreat my readers to obtain this bargain box before it becomes history.tion that is as close to \\"definitve\\" as any recorded Bach Cantata I know. B000002G6J|5.0|There are different forms of Brazilian music, just as there are many colors in the American pop music palette. Few people like all approaches to a music genre.  But, just about anyone interested in Brazilian music can find something to enjoy in the work of Antonio Carlos Jobim.  This LP is a jazz-bossa nova classic, arranged by Claus Ogerman, who really knew how to evoke a breezy beauty when working with strings.  Mr. Ogerman also arranged the excellent Sinatra-Jobim album.  This is a somewhat understated but very rhythmic work that has an atmosphere reminiscent of a trip to St. Maarten or Antigua.  Somehow, you just feel like you're in the deep Carribean when listening to this album.  The musicianship is excellent: Jimmy Cleveland and Urbie Green on trombones and a group of fine violinists whose names you will know if you read album covers.  Recorded by the eminent engineer, Rudy Van Gelder, it's essential for anyone interested in Jobim's music. I recall that this LP received a fair amount of play on the local jazz station when it was released ca. 1967.  But, it's not straight jazz.  It's Jobim at his best.  To steal a phrase from an old TV commercial: Try it, you'll like it!

B0071T5PN0|4.0|28 years have passed since Van Halen last released an album with David Lee Roth on the microphone - the 10 million selling juggernaut 1984.  In the decade following Roth's departure, Van Halen released 4 highly successful albums with Sammy Hagar earning a legion of new fans who embraced the band's more polished song writing and pop sensibility, but alienating those who felt the band had abandoned its southern California hard rock roots.  Throw in the departure of original bassist Michael Anthony (a gifted vocalist with a keen ear for harmony) and you have a band competing fiercely with its past.  Perhaps that's why the band intentionally decided to embrace its history and dig through its archives for inspiration.  Roughly half the album is reworked demos from the band's earliest days, many of which appeared on a Gene Simmons financed demo in 1976.  In many ways the album is a gift to the long-time, die hard fans who stuck it out through all of the band acrimony over a decade of false starts - a brief stint with Extreme's Gary Cherone, 2 failed attempts to reunite with Roth and a half-hearted tour with Sammy Hagar in 2004 that put Eddie's battle with alcohol in full view.  The song \\"Blood and Fire\\" is a nod to the band's rocky past and a salute to the fans - \\"look at all the people here tonight\\" Roth croons in a gesture that is certain to illicit roars from a live audience.  It is the album's most realized track, featuring a catchy melody from Roth and a solo from Eddie that is as accomplished and thrilling as anything in the band's catalog. \\"Blood and Fire\\" and the rather underwhelming first single, \\"Tattoo,\\" prove to be the album's only nods to Van Halen's softer side.  It only takes one spin of A Different Kind of Truth to realize that the band aims to remind you that they were once America's greatest rock band.  Overall, this is a collection of tracks that has much more in common with Fair Warning than it does 1984. \\"As Is,\\"  \\"China Town,\\"  \\"Bullethead,\\" \\" Honeybabysweetie Doll\\" and \\"Outta Space\\" are as heavy as anything the band has ever recorded.  These tunes are sure to please fans of very hard rock and wouldn't be out of place should the band ever play the Sonisphere Festival.  They rock and rock hard. The tempos are furiously fast, putting Alex Van Halen's impressive chops to full use for the first time since \\"Hot for Teacher.\\" Wolfgang Van Halen not only holds his own, he shines.  That said, fans of the melodic pop hooks of both the Roth and Hagar eras (e.g., \\"Jump,\\" \\" Why Can't this Be Love,\\" \\"Dreams,\\" \\" Dance the Night Away\\") are going to be, understandably, scratching their heads during parts of the album.  The heavier material in particular is long on ambition but short on melody and song craft.  Roth has always been more showman than singer, more wordsmith than master of melody.  He talks his way through a little too much of the record for most tastes, often choosing to force lyrics into small spaces when brevity and simplicity might have served the song better.  That's not to say that the album is without hooks.  \\"She's the Woman,\\" \\" Big River,\\" \\" You and Your Blues,\\" \\"The Trouble with Never\\" and \\"Beats Workin'\\" all feature the type of  memorable blues rock grooves and soaring harmonies that make so much of the Van Halen catalog winning.  \\"Stay Frosty\\" is a rollicking ZZ Top-style boogie that harkens back to the acoustic blues to rock explosions Ice Cream Man, Take Your Whiskey Home and The Full Bug.  Anthony's vocals are missed, but Wolfgang, Eddie(and perhaps some help from modern studio technology) at the very least approximate the band's signature harmonies.  If this were Van Halen's first album instead of the 7th, Eddie Van Halen's singular combination of blues feel and innovative guitar acrobatics would still launch a thousand imitators.  It really sounds that fresh.  And what Roth lacks in vocal chops, he more than makes up for with intent, youthful exuberance and a healthy sense of irony.  A Different Kind of Truth is a solid effort that is worthy of the band's legacy and a just reward to its loyal (and very patient) fan base.

0143119680|3.0|When I saw that this book was almost 600 pages long and the author was a historian, I was afraid that it might get bogged down in historical dissertation. Not to worry! Historical references are very brief and general, disappointingly so in some cases: I really would have liked to know more about alchemical imagery and its meanings, for instance. The problem, in fact, proved to be not too much history, but too much romance. Most of the book is spent establishing, over and over and OVER AND OVER, that the main characters, reluctant witch Diana and handsome vampire Matthew, are deeply attracted to each other. Although these characters did grow on me after a while, I didn't find either of them that interesting, especially Matthew. The writing isn't bad; it did keep me going through all those pages. I liked some of the secondary characters, too, including Matthew's mother, Ysabeau, and Diana's Aunt Sarah and her partner, Em (and their sentient, opinionated house!). They don't do much until the second half of the book, though. The book does pick up its pace in these later stages... only to end just as something interesting is finally getting ready to happen. At that point it seemed like the entire six hundred pages had been nothing more than a much-too-long prelude to whatever the author plans for a sequel, which left me feeling cheated and highly irritated. There's nothing wrong with sequels, but each book should also be able to stand on its own, and this one ultimately does not.

B003XF1XOQ|3.0|I was looking forward to wrapping up the Hunger Games Trilogy, and just wondered how Collins was going to end her incredible series.<br /><br />The first 75% of the books was action-packed. A little gory, as usual, and just fast paced. I was really happy with how this book just took off from the beginning and wouldn't let go. Catching Fire, on the other hand, started slow and got better as it went along.<br /><br />Mockingjay started out fantastic, and ended with a ho-hum from me. I think the weakness of First Person Point of View is highlighted in the last 25% of this book. Without giving away too much, think about this - if your narrator is \\"out of commission\\" for whatever reason (knocked out, asleep, etc.), then your story pretty much is limited only to that character's waking moments.<br /><br />Apparently, a whole lot happened that the reader never gets to experience. With First Person, the writer is unable to weave subplots together, and secondary characters might get left behind or underdeveloped. Collins did an excellent job with these two innate weaknesses in the POV she chose to write in, but that final quarter of Mockingjay suffers terribly from being limited to only Katniss' perspective.<br /><br />Just a whole lot of telling instead of showing, which spells BORING. Some might hate the book because of the actual ending - but I think it's quite clever. The best way to end a book and make sure your fans are emotionally exploding at the end of your series is to give them a not-so-liked ending. So I don't have an issue with that at all.

The game never gets frustratingly difficult, and I played for hours before my phone needed a recharge.  The game does have some flaws that impact my score for it, and they're mostly due to the interface. For starters, there are no tilt controls. In this type of game, tilt controls would benefit steering the squirrel greatly. Instead, you're stuck using the left and right sides of the screen for input. Since you can select power ups while flying, tilt control would freed up your fingers to use them instead of having to choose between flying left or using a crash helmet. You can't set anything up from a main options screen; everything is scattered. For example, you can't turn off the sounds or music from anywhere but in the flying part of the game, and you can't opt out of Open Feint till after it logs you in and starts collecting data. I really don't like OF, but at least other games ask if you want to use it instead of just using it without asking. Even checking achievements loads OF. Why couldn't this just be done in-game? Lastly, the power ups seem sparse. The squirrel has a very bad turn speed, so I was hoping there would at least be an outfit or gear that increased agility or speed, but all the outfits appear to be only cosmetic, and the gear focuses on nut collection, distance boosting, and collision with obstacles.  Again, I really enjoy this game. I haven't had any freeze-ups or crashes in it like many other games do, and the game is really fun to play. The stars off from 5 are -1 for poorly-designed interface, and -1 for no up-front opt out of Open Feint. It's really a shame there are so many simple-to-fix problems with the interface that could have made this a 5-star game in my book.  UPDATE: On my third time playing the game I was once again logged into Open Feint involuntarily. This was the third time I've had to delete the OF account because the game started it without my input other than launching the game. The game also asked me to rate it twice in one game. Because of the Open Feint problem and the constant rating nag screens I've deemed this game unfit to remain on my phone. Unless the developers start hearing the cries of their customers and remove the nag screens and Open Feint problem this game will not get anything past 1 star from me (down from 3 before the update to this review).

They have to sacrifice a boy and girl each year to participate in lethal hunger games - where only one victor may reign. Thse hunger games serve two purposes: to keep the provinces in fear and remind them who's boss (the capital with its technological prowess) and secondly to provide entertainment to the desensitized and out-of-touch capital residents. The fierce Everdeen volunteers herself to participate and so begin the feverish and thrilling hunger games. B005NPLXJ4|1.0|Regardless of whether you have a religious bone in your body or not, you have to admit vanity is one bad sin when it leaves albums like this in its wake.<br /><br />First off, I see some reviewers on here claiming that Lulu is so bad that it will likely develop a cult status in 20 years much the same way Metal Machine Music has in recent times. I would like to take the time to nip that comparison in the bud by simply saying that MMM has some redeeming qualities. I dare anyone to try to think during any part of the one hour long duration of the tuneless layers of guitar feedback. It is literally impossible to maintain a working brain during that time and this is a good thing for it gives the brain a chance to reset and start all over. I highly recommend [[ASIN:B00004VXF2 Metal Machine Music]] to anyone who has trouble dwelling on negative thoughts for the album will stop them cold for a full hour and do so without a single chemical entering your body. This album required thought and in this sense it has zero redeeming qualities to it. Lulu is closer to Reed's the Raven.<br /><br />Lulu lacks any kind of cohesion. There's Reed (obviously showing his age) singing and falling short on telling any kind of meaningful story because the music doesn't fit the mood. Metallica isn't helping matters by making the music louder than it probably should be considering the subject matter. I'd think a Reed/Metallica album would be about drug fueled fast living and a fiery burnout. Fast moving, fast living and predictably abrupt tragic endings, it's something all musicians involved witnessed and survived in their lives. Lulu, is a miserable character, low self esteem, driven even lower by circumstance and as much as I try to ignore the lyrics because they don't fit the music being played behind them, I can't. The question is, WHAT WERE THEY THINKING? WERE THEY THINKING? Or was this a Metallica jam session behind a Reed written piece he had lying around?<br /><br />Reed fans like me have reason to be sad but the sadness also carries over to any Metallica fan who still cherishes the Master of Puppets era of this once great band. I can see one of these fans taking his CD copy of Lulu and running it over with his '86 GMC Jimmy repeatedly, for days until it at least looked like a cool disc to have. If you are a fan of vintage Metallica (anything pre-Load because Load was a LOAD imo)  DON'T BUY LULU. There is nothing on here you will like. The band likely chose to do this album simply because they thought it would be cool to jam with the Great Grandfather of Alternative Rock N Roll. That my friends makes Lulu a vanity project and these endeavors almost never amount to anything for us fans except heartache.<br /><br />So if you are a fan of Metallica, please do not see this as a slap in the face. If you must fill the hole in your collection, get this with the instrumental tracks instead of the storyline Reed tries to stick to it.<br /><br />On the whole I feel this is just another example of a band who is on a very long losing streak. She teases us with the names as she dives into Katniss' life.  The story is written in present tense, which is hard to pull off for a complete novel.  Collins handled perfectly.  After the first few sentences, I didn't even notice the different tense anymore.  The world of the Hunger Games is dreary, it is broken, and yet fascinating.  Collins describes it well, putting me into that world and letting me marvel in it without drowning me in needless paragraphs of description.  The concept of the story is fascinating, the characters even more so.  There's romance - but it's subtle, it's confused, and not in the slightest cheesy.  Katniss is independent and strong, a wonderful protagonist.  All in all, I was thrilled by this book.  I can't wait to get my hands on the next ones.

A guitar solo or song breakdown is in the same place in nearly every single song. There were no pleasant surprises. Everything was just too predictable. I even bought the digital version specifically for the three additional songs that were not included on the hard-copy release of The Unforgiving, and yet I find myself skipping over more than half of the 15 songs when I listen to the album. Even the songs I've become fond of are not on my list of Within Temptation favorites.<br /><br />My two biggest problems with \\"The Unforgiving\\" are the lyrics and the song compositions. Sharon's voice is still polished and, as ever, improving in strength and range. The band plays with skill and experience. The Unforgiving is a concept album based on a companion comic book series, but without the comic to supplement, the songs don't actually tell an intelligible story. They don't stand on their own. One literally NEEDS the comic book to understand what the album is trying to convey. It's an interesting marketing ploy but unfortunately turns the album into a glorified commercial. I don't want to be sold a comic. I want a good piece of music that I can enjoy.<br /><br />I count Within Temptation among my favorite bands and only hope that their next album it isn't as disappointing as The Unforgiving.only hope that their next album it isn't as disappointing as The Unforgiving.

B005LLX582|5.0|The Tree of Life is not only a movie, but an experience. It's a visually stunning, deeply moving and perfectly acted film. And if you know what to expect, you may enjoy it as much as I did.<br /><br />But be warned. The Tree of Life does not have a linear narrative, and doesn't tell a story as much as it shows it. And the regular viewer--that is, most of us--, used to be told in images and words what we are supposed to see and feel, may find this story hard to follow.<br /><br />The movie starts with a flaming light and a quotation from the Book of Job: \\"Where were you when I laid the earth's foundation...while the morning stars sang together and all the sons of God shouted for joy?\\"<br /><br />Then we are shown a woman (Jessica Chastain) as she learns in a letter the death of her son, age 19. In her grief and despair, enhanced by the absence of sound in the subsequent images, the mother questions God's design in such a seemingly cruel act.<br /><br />From here, the movie proceeds in two alternating levels. One shows the creation of the universe, the gathering of dust that made the planets, the apparition of microscopic life, scenes from the time of the dinosaurs and the destruction of most life on Earth as a consequence of a meteor hitting our planet.<br /><br />In another level we are shown images from the life of a contemporary middle aged man (Sean Penn): his birth in the 1950s to a loving mother (Jessica Chastain), his growing up in Texas under a strict father (Brad Pitt), his rebellion as an adolescent and his relationship with his two younger brothers. The second, the one he is closer to, will die later at 19 as we learned at the beginning of the movie.<br /><br />The story unfolds in pieces and bits, the way our memory works: some scenes from our past are vividly clear while others are lost. The overall picture that unfolds is both that of a unique human being and an universal story.<br /><br />If the movie sounds dreadfully boring, it's my fault. The Tree of Life maybe slow but it's never boring.<br /><br />So, in conclusion, if you want an adrenaline rush, a fast moving thriller or a light comedy, don't watch The Tree of Life. But if you want to take a break from a too stressful life and step in a world made out of memories and dreams. If you are ready to be moved and awed and inspired, then this movie is perfect for you.f Life maybe slow but it's never boring.<br /><br />So, in conclusion, if you want an adrenaline rush, a fast moving thriller or a light comedy, don't watch The Tree of Life. But if you want to take a break from a too stressful life and step in a world made out of memories and dreams. If you are ready to be moved and awed and inspired, then this movie is perfect for you.

B004MPRWJG|2.0|The Lovely Bones has sold a million plus copies since its release about a decade ago and I finally got around to reading it. This is not a bad novel for fans of light reads(yes, this a light read even though the story is about a murdered child). The language is very simplistic and there is a clear cut desire to make sure that every plot point is neatly resolved. Also, the constant reminders that Susie Salmon is narrating from her personal heaven helps to offset some of the horror that a reader may feel regarding the violence. The novel has some interesting parts when showing how Suzie's loss affected her family and the different ways they deal with the loss. However, this becomes dull after a while as the loss is never expanded or elaborated on past the most obvious and blatant effects. Maybe this was intentional on Ms Sebold's part as the narrator is perennially 13 years old. Characters age in the novel and the reader watches them go through many different life stages but the they are never fully developed and sometimes the interactions between characters can seem overly simplistic and unnatural. Most frustrating to me was the cleanness of every resolution to every plot point, each plot point had to resolved in such a way that there was no room for interpretation and  that every conflict ended happily. In conclusion, this is a good book for young readers at the Jr High level (which I believe was the author's intended audience) and for a casual reader looking for a quick read. If you are a more serious reader this book is one to avoid simply because it has little to offer the more selective or critical reader.

0307474259|1.0|After hearing about Taubes' theories about exercise and weight loss (he doesn't believe exercise is an effective method of weight loss) through a friend, I took particular interest in reading these sections in both his \\"Good Calories Bad Calories\\" book and in \\"Why We Get Fat\\". As a new runner, I was specifically interested in the 2 \\"runner\\" studies he mentions. I took the time to read through the actual study papers he referenced to see if Taubes was providing a one-sided view, and if the studies themselves were valid. My conclusion: Taubes draws very convenient, but mostly invalid, conclusions from the studies. The studies he references are HUGELY flawed in that they do not control for caloric intake, they do not account for changes in body composition vs. weight loss, and they do not consider the fact that the studies start with healthy, non-obese individuals with healthy BMIs and bodyfat %-- not exactly the population to use when looking for the effectiveness of exercise on weight loss, is it?  Here's what I found:  1. Study referenced in \\"Good Calories Bad Calories\\" and \\"Why We Get Fat\\"- \\"Food intake and body composition in novice athletes during a training period to run a marathon\\"- Taubs used this to imply that not even marathon training is useful to weight loss:  - While the participants in this study were initially sedentary, they were not initially obese- and all had normal bodyfat readings (16% bf male, 24% bf female) when starting the study. Exercise will not continually cause fat loss to 0%- it will reach an equilibirum, and the participants in this study appear to have already been at a healthy weight / BF %, so this is not a good study to determine whether exercise leads to weight (and esp fat) loss in obese people or others who need to lose weight.  - Taubes states average weight loss for men was 5lbs. If he wanted to use a round number, 6lbs would have been more accurate (5.95lbs). More importantly, that's pretty decent weight loss for men with an average of 16% bodyfat to start with, but somehow this fact is used to imply the weight loss was not \\"enough\\". Again, these men were in a healthy bodyfat range, not obese, so why isn't this taken into consideration?  - The study reports no significant weight loss in women, but it does show loss in bodyfat in women, and it does not account for potential gains in muscle mass, which may explain the lack of weight loss  - Taubes refers to \\"body fat loss\\" in lbs, but the study measures and reports absolute weight loss in lbs instead- it reports body fat %, but not body fat loss in lbs.  - Study does not control/account for:  - Body composition changes- study reports weight loss without accounting for body composition changes (e.g., gaining X lbs of muscle, losing X lbs of fat may be a better result than simply losing X lbs of fat, but it is instead viewed as \\"lack of results of exercise for weight loss\\" by Taubes). Considering that \\"unhealthy weight\\" and \\"fat loss\\" are the key factors when discussing weight loss, this is misleading at best.  - Physical activity outside running  - Most importantly- study does not control for calories consumed! So runners are eating more when running (as stated in the study), and they are not losing a lot of weight, big surprise?  2. Study referenced in \\"Why we get fat\\": \\"The effects of changing exercise levels on weight and age-related weight gain\\"  Williams/Wood study. Taubes uses this study to imply that, not only is exercise ineffective when it comes to weight loss, but those that do exercise gain a \\"tolerance\\" to it than requires more and more exercise to produce the same weight loss results. Once again, a flawed take on a flawed study.  - The participants in this study were not obese- and all had normal BMIs when starting the study. Exercise will not continually cause fat/weight loss to 0- it will reach an equilibirum, and the participants in this study appear to have already been at healthy BMIs, so this is not a good study to determine whether exercise leads to weight (and esp fat) loss in people who need to lose weight- this was a study of a population of regular runners with normal BMIs.  - Taubes does not mention key points in the study that do not agree with his theory:  - \\"Changes in running distance were inversely related to changes in men's and women's bmis (slope¬±se: -0.015¬±0.001 and - 13 0.009¬±0.001 kg/m2 per *km/wk, respectively)\\"  - \\"Our data lend essential support for the hypothesis that vigorous 5 exercise promotes leanness. Because our analyses are based on changing levels of exercise, the associations are unlikely to arise from lean men and women choosing to run.\\"  - Taubes implies some sort of \\"immunity\\" to exercise that comes with a longer history of running, but he does not consider:  - Caloric intake is not controlled for (big one!)  - Weight/bodyfat may reach a healthy equilibirum, so little change may be seen in people with a long history of running  - Age-related metabolic slowing (common knowledge) which is shown in study: \\"when adjusted for *km/wk, years of aging in men and 21 years of aging in women were associated with increases of 22 0.066¬±0.005 and 0.056¬±0.005 kg/m2 in bmi, respectively, increases23 of 0.294¬±0.019 and 0.279¬±0.028% in *%body weight, respectively, 24 and increases of 0.203¬±0.016 and 0.271¬±0.032 cm in waist 25 circumference, respectively (all p<0.0001)\\" What about comparing this rate of weight gain with sedentary people? And again, what about caloric intake?  - Study does not control/account for:  - Body composition changes- study reports weight loss without accounting for body composition changes (e.g., gaining X lbs of muscle, losing X lbs of fat may be a better result than simply losing X lbs of fat, but it is instead viewed as \\"lack of results of exercise for weight loss\\" by Taubes). Considering that \\"unhealthy weight\\" and \\"fat loss\\" are the key factors when discussing weight loss, this is misleading at best.  - Physical activity outside running  - Most importantly- study does not control for calories consumed! So runners may be eating more when running, and they are not losing a lot of weight, big surprise? As the study authors state themselves: \\"our surveys lacked reliable data on changes in energy intake and other sources of energy expenditure that could account for some of the results reported here. Some of the change in body weight could reflect changes in caloric intake and other activities.\\"  3. Taubes states several times (ex pg. 37 why we get fat) his belief that exercise leads to increased caloric intake, but does not examine what happens if calories are controlled (i.e., exercise will lead to weight loss if caloric intake is controlled instead of increased when exercising).  Again, Taubes is twisting facts to fit his narrow view. Why doesn't Taubes reference studies that control or account for factors like caloric intake, BMI/bodyfat of study participants, and body composition changes? Maybe because they wouldn't agree with his viewpoint. I hope the pseudo-science presented in Taubes' \\"Exercise vs. Weightloss\\" claims aren't representative of the rest of the book, but the fact that he doesn't even mention these factors is enough for me to give the book 1 star based on this section alone. Taubes is not a scientist, a doctor, or even a nutritionist. He's a journalist. And it shows.

There are also passages written by Taya, Mr. Kyle's wife, where she would discuss everything from the day they met at a bar to how she was lonely raising one and then two children while Mr. Kyle was deployed. Maybe she and Mr. Kyle were two of the very few people on the face of the planet that did not know about the high divorce rate in the SEAL ranks and how tough it would be to raise a family with a spouse that would need to spend so much time in training or deployed with his team. After the first couple of Taya's passages, I found them to be annoying.  The book is not a bad read, I just think it could have been better. I have read reviews here that indicate it is a poorly written book -- I do not think this is the case. I remember noticing only one misspelling and felt that the narrative was not bad -- just boring.

The first set we had, the sync problem extended from the Ron Returns portion of the film through the beach scene. With the same problem in the same spot on the same disk (though slightly less duration the second time) I can only conclude there is a bad batch of these. I very much hope that the manufacturer simply had a bad day in quality control. I would hate to think they are passing these off in the multi-disk set.  My kids love these, and I hate to return them. But even as cheap as they are (~ $10 per disk) it's just not worth it when one of the movies is rendered unwatchable.

1594203334|4.0|I found this book thoroughly enjoyable. Rather a how-to book or a bland parenting book, it's written more like a novel that follows the author on her journey as a mother raising her children in a foreign culture. While I was sometimes frustrated with the lack of practical \\"how-to\\" explanations of the various principles that the author lays out, the book is so well written (she's a former foreign correspondent for a major NY newspaper - perhaps the NY TImes? And it shows in the caliber of her writing) that I was willing to forgive this.  There were lots of little nuggets throughout the book that I tucked away for future use with my toddler (don't overpraise, give them firm boundaries but let them be free within them, require respectful behavior to adults), I found myself asking a lot \\"then what?\\" Perhaps it's all the brie and red wine that French women drink that breeds docile children but it sure seemed like the children never challenged any of these principles or rules. There was a frustrating lack of ideas on what to do when your child challenges these rules (or the \\"cadre\\" as its called in French). For example, the author tells a story of how her 10 month old daughter pulls all of the books off the bookshelf. Assuming this is normal, she jsut picks them up later. But her French friends say that she should rationally explain the proper behavior and simply expect compliance. Her friend visits and says to the baby, \\"We don't pull books. See, gently on the book shelf\\" and puts them back up. Her daughter not only helps put them away but doesn't pull them off again. Long before reading this book I'd tried that tactic with my 12-month old son. He looked at me, laughed and kept pulling the books off the shelf. Then what?  In another example, she relates \\"The Pause\\" - a principle where French parents allow a baby to cry or fuss for about 5-8 minutes before they tend to her, teaching hte baby to wait. This principle supposedly helps French babies self-soothe and they sleepthrough the night much earlier. Again, long before reading this book I was familiar with the concept of self-soothing and aimed to teach my infant son this skill. I did my version of \\"The Pause\\" for five or ten minutes before tending to him middle of the night, and by seven months he still wasn't sleeping through. He would have \\"Paused\\" until he was six years old if I let him. Then what?  But despite the lack of in depth step-by-step instructions, the book still was entertaining, refreshing, and enlightening. B0067LY4WG|4.0|Yes, I'll freely admit it: Along with being a fan of Gustav Mahler, Dmitri Shostakovich, Tony Bennett and Jack Sheldon (how's that for eclectic?), I've also been turned on to Leonard Cohen - the great Canadian alternative to Bob Dylan (sorry, not a fan). In his current 'late' state, Cohen's voice has become ever more baritone like. As a commentator on our society - and on life in general - Cohen strikes me as being somewhere between Dylan and Tom Waits. He has Dylan's eye for detail, but with a bit of Tom Wait's tongue-in-cheek sense of humor. Like all great dandies, he's a former 'great lover' who probably can't get away with any of the wild things he used to get away with (I hope I'm wrong about that). Leonard Cohen knows it, and knows how to express it in a poetic and dignified way. He's not just another old-age rocker trying to pretend that he's still 25 years old.  \\"Old Ideas\\" is definitely a seminal work. It's hard to imagine that Cohen will have many more albums after this one, if any. Like Shostakovich's 15th and final String Quartet, all of the songs are slow in tempo. In addition, only a couple of these new tunes build to any kind of climax. But Cohen's voice is deep, soft and close-up on the microphone. This superbly mixed CD is accompanied by only the sparsest instrumentation, along with the Webb Sisters providing the usual background vocals - vocals that make a great contrast to Cohen's continued groveling in the depths. But I what also notice this time out, is just how much there's a Southern, almost Gospel-like quality to many of Cohen's more recent songs. Even some of the background and solo instruments suggest that, such as the banjo. That tinge of southern spice does jive nicely with Cohen's deepest ruminations.  If you're already a fan of Leonard Cohen, I'm convinced that \\"Old Ideas\\" will grow on you with time. I certainly liked it more the second time through. Those who are not familiar with Cohen should probably sample this first, to see if theslow tempos and old-age ruminations strike a chord at all (no pun intended). Otherwise, start off with some earlier material first.

They have tapped the proverbial fountain of youth with all the fire and flash of the old days with a BIG, in your face modern production sound that sounds so good, you will not miss the reverb drenched days of old. So since you've heard it all already, I'll just give my .02c on the tracks...  1.
